id="mw-head-base" class="noprint"></div>\n\t\t<div id="content" class="mw-body" role="main">\n\t\t\t<a id="top"></a>\n\t\t\t<div id="siteNotice" class="mw-body-content"><!-- CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n<div id="mw-indicator-featured-star" class="mw-indicator"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Featured_articles" title="This is a featured article. Click here for more information."><img alt="This is a featured article. Click here for more information." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Cscr-featured.svg/20px-Cscr-featured.svg.png" width="20" height="19" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Cscr-featured.svg/30px-Cscr-featured.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Cscr-featured.svg/40px-Cscr-featured.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="462" data-file-height="438" /></a></div>\n</div>\n<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Scotland in the High Middle Ages</h1>\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"></div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DUNNOTTAR_CASTLE_Large.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/DUNNOTTAR_CASTLE_Large.JPG/300px-DUNNOTTAR_CASTLE_Large.JPG" width="300" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/DUNNOTTAR_CASTLE_Large.JPG/450px-DUNNOTTAR_CASTLE_Large.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/DUNNOTTAR_CASTLE_Large.JPG/600px-DUNNOTTAR_CASTLE_Large.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="750" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DUNNOTTAR_CASTLE_Large.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dunnottar_Castle" title="Dunnottar Castle">Dunnottar Castle</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Kincardineshire" title="Kincardineshire">Mearns</a> occupies one of the best defensive locations in Great Britain. The site was in use throughout the High Middle Ages, and the castle itself dates to the fourteenth century.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <b>High Middle Ages of Scotland</b> encompass <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> in the era between the death of <a href="/wiki/Donald_II_of_Scotland" title="Donald II of Scotland">Domnall II</a> in 900 AD and the death of King <a href="/wiki/Alexander_III_of_Scotland" title="Alexander III of Scotland">Alexander III</a> in 1286, which was an indirect cause of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Wars_of_Independence" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Wars of Independence">Scottish Wars of Independence</a>.\n</p><p>At the close of the ninth century, various competing kingdoms occupied the territory of modern Scotland. <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavian</a> influence was dominant in the northern and western islands, <a href="/wiki/Britons_(historical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (historical)">Brythonic</a> culture in the southwest, the Anglo-Saxon or English <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Northumbria" title="Kingdom of Northumbria">Kingdom of Northumbria</a> in the southeast and the <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Pictish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a> <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Alba" title="Kingdom of Alba">Kingdom of Alba</a> in the east, north of the <a href="/wiki/River_Forth" title="River Forth">River Forth</a>. By the tenth and eleventh centuries, northern <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> was increasingly dominated by Gaelic culture, and by the Gaelic regal lordship of <i><a href="/wiki/Alba" title="Alba">Alba</a></i>, known in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> as either <i>Albania</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Scotia" title="Scotia">Scotia</a></i>, and in <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> as "Scotland". From its base in the east, this kingdom acquired control of the lands lying to the south and ultimately the west and much of the north. It had a flourishing culture, comprising part of the larger Gaelic-speaking world and an economy dominated by agriculture and trade.\n</p><p>After the twelfth-century reign of <a href="/wiki/David_I_of_Scotland" title="David I of Scotland">King David I</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_monarchs" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish monarchs">Scottish monarchs</a> are better described as <a href="/wiki/Scoto-Norman" title="Scoto-Norman">Scoto-Norman</a> than Gaelic, preferring <a href="/wiki/French_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="French culture">French culture</a> to native Scottish culture. A consequence was the spread of French institutions and social values including <a href="/wiki/Canon_law_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Canon law (Catholic Church)">Canon law</a>. The first towns, called <a href="/wiki/Burghs" class="mw-redirect" title="Burghs">burghs</a>, appeared in the same era, and as they spread, so did the <a href="/wiki/Middle_English_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle English language">Middle English language</a>. These developments were offset by the acquisition of the <a href="/wiki/Norse-Gaels" class="mw-redirect" title="Norse-Gaels">Norse-Gaelic</a> west and the <a href="/wiki/Gaelicisation" title="Gaelicisation">Gaelicisation</a> of many of the noble families of <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a> and <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Anglo-French</a> origin. National cohesion was fostered with the creation of various unique religious and cultural practices. By the end of the period, Scotland experienced a "Gaelic revival", which created an integrated <a href="/wiki/Scottish_national_identity" title="Scottish national identity">Scottish national identity</a>. By 1286, these economic, institutional, cultural, religious and legal developments had brought Scotland closer to its neighbours in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">the Continent</a>, although outsiders continued to view Scotland as a provincial, even savage place. By this date, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Kingdom of Scotland</a> had political boundaries that closely resembled those of the modern nation.\n</p>\n<table class="toctext">Secondary sources</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Historiography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> in the High Middle Ages is a relatively well-studied topic and Scottish medievalists have produced a wide variety of publications. Some, such as <a href="/wiki/David_Dumville" title="David Dumville">David Dumville</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Owen_Clancy" title="Thomas Owen Clancy">Thomas Owen Clancy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dauvit_Broun" title="Dauvit Broun">Dauvit Broun</a>, are primarily interested in the native cultures of the country, and often have linguistic training in the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Normanists, such as <a href="/wiki/G.W.S._Barrow" class="mw-redirect" title="G.W.S. Barrow">G.W.S. Barrow</a>, are concerned with the <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Norman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scoto-Norman" title="Scoto-Norman">Scoto-Norman</a> cultures introduced to Scotland after the eleventh century. For much of the twentieth century, historians tended to stress the cultural change that took place in Scotland during this time.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> However, scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Cynthia_Neville" title="Cynthia Neville">Cynthia Neville</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Oram" title="Richard Oram">Richard Oram</a>, while not ignoring cultural changes, argue that continuity with the <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a> past was just as, if not more, important.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since the publication of <i>Scandinavian Scotland</i> by Barbara E. Crawford in 1987, there has been a growing volume of work dedicated to the understanding of Norse influence in this period. However, from 849 on, when <a href="/wiki/Columba" title="Columba">Columba</a>\'s relics were removed from <a href="/wiki/Iona" title="Iona">Iona</a> in the face of Viking incursions, written evidence from local sources in the areas under <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavian</a> influence all but vanishes for three hundred years.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The sources for information about the <a href="/wiki/Hebrides" title="Hebrides">Hebrides</a> and indeed much of northern Scotland from the eighth to the eleventh century, are thus almost exclusively Irish, English or Norse. The main Norse texts were written in the early thirteenth century and should be treated with care. The English and Irish sources are more contemporary, but according to historian <a href="/wiki/Alex_Woolf" title="Alex Woolf">Alex Woolf</a>, may have "led to a southern bias in the story", especially as much of the Hebridean archipelago became Norse-speaking during this period.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are various traditional clan histories dating from the nineteenth century such as the "monumental" <i>The Clan Donald</i><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> and a significant corpus of material from the Gaelic oral tradition that relates to this period, although their value is questionable.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_of_the_Kingdom_of_Alba">Origins of the Kingdom of Alba</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins of the Kingdom of Alba">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Origins_of_the_Kingdom_of_Alba" title="Origins of the Kingdom of Alba">Origins of the Kingdom of Alba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_Scotland" title="Scandinavian Scotland">Scandinavian Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Forres_sueno.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Forres_sueno.jpg/170px-Forres_sueno.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Forres_sueno.jpg/255px-Forres_sueno.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Forres_sueno.jpg/340px-Forres_sueno.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Forres_sueno.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sueno%27s_Stone" title="Sueno&#39;s Stone">Sueno\'s Stone</a> Located in <a href="/wiki/Forres" title="Forres">Forres</a>, in the old kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Fortriu" title="Fortriu">Fortriu</a>, this gigantic probably post-Pictish monument marks some kind of military triumph</div></div></div>\n<p>At the close of the ninth century various polities occupied Scotland. The <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Pictish</a> and Gaelic <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Alba" title="Kingdom of Alba">Kingdom of Alba</a> had just been united in the east; the Scandinavian-influenced <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_the_Isles" title="Kingdom of the Isles">Kingdom of the Isles</a> emerged in the west. <a href="/wiki/Ragnall_ua_%C3%8Dmair" title="Ragnall ua \xc3\x8dmair">Ragnall ua \xc3\x8dmair</a> was a key figure at this time although the extent to which he ruled territory in western and northern Scotland including the Hebrides and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Isles" title="Northern Isles">Northern Isles</a> is unknown as contemporary sources are silent on this matter.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dumbarton_Castle" title="Dumbarton Castle">Dumbarton</a>, the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Strathclyde" title="Kingdom of Strathclyde">Kingdom of Strathclyde</a> had been sacked by the <a href="/wiki/U%C3%AD_%C3%8Dmair" title="U\xc3\xad \xc3\x8dmair">U\xc3\xad \xc3\x8dmair</a> in 870.<sup id="cite_ref-W109_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W109-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> This was clearly a major assault, which may have brought the whole of mainland Scotland under temporary U\xc3\xad Imair control.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;Note 1&#93;</a></sup> The south-east had been absorbed by the <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">English</a> <a href="/wiki/Bernicia" title="Bernicia">Kingdom of Bernicia/Northumbria</a> in the seventh century. <a href="/wiki/Galloway" title="Galloway">Galloway</a> in the south west was a Lordship with some regality. In a <a href="/wiki/Galwegian_Gaelic" title="Galwegian Gaelic">Galwegian</a> charter dated to the reign of <a href="/wiki/Fergus_of_Galloway" title="Fergus of Galloway">Fergus</a>, the Galwegian ruler styled himself <i>rex Galwitensium</i>, King of Galloway.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In the north east the <a href="/wiki/Mormaer_of_Moray" title="Mormaer of Moray">ruler of Moray</a> was called not only "king" in both Scandinavian and Irish sources, but before <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A1el_Snechtai_of_Moray" title="M\xc3\xa1el Snechtai of Moray">M\xc3\xa1el Snechtai</a>, "King of Alba".<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, when <a href="/wiki/Donald_II_of_Scotland" title="Donald II of Scotland">Domnall mac Causant\xc3\xadn</a> died at <a href="/wiki/Dunnotar_Castle" class="mw-redirect" title="Dunnotar Castle">Dunnottar</a> in 900, he was the first man to be recorded as <i>r\xc3\xad Alban</i><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and his kingdom was the nucleus that would expand as Viking and other influences waned. In the tenth century the <a href="/wiki/Alba" title="Alba">Alban</a> elite had begun to develop a conquest myth to explain their increasing <a href="/wiki/Gaelicisation" title="Gaelicisation">Gaelicisation</a> at the expense of Pictish culture. Known as <a href="/wiki/MacAlpin%27s_treason" title="MacAlpin&#39;s treason">MacAlpin\'s Treason</a>, it describes how <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_I_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Kenneth I of Scotland">Cin\xc3\xa1ed mac Ailp\xc3\xadn</a> is supposed to have annihilated the Picts in one fell takeover.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;Note 2&#93;</a></sup> However, modern historians are now beginning to reject this conceptualization of Scottish origins.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> No contemporary sources mention this conquest. Moreover, the Gaelicisation of Pictland was a long process predating Cin\xc3\xa1ed, and is evidenced by Gaelic-speaking Pictish rulers,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Pictish royal patronage of Gaelic poets,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> and Gaelic inscriptions<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> and placenames.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The change of identity can perhaps be explained by the death of the <a href="/wiki/Pictish_language" title="Pictish language">Pictish language</a>, but also important may be <a href="/wiki/Constantine_II_of_Scotland" title="Constantine II of Scotland">Causant\xc3\xadn II</a>\'s alleged Scoticisation of the "Pictish" Church<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> and the trauma caused by <a href="/wiki/Viking" class="mw-redirect" title="Viking">Viking</a> invasions, most strenuously felt in the Pictish kingdom\'s heartland of <a href="/wiki/Fortriu" title="Fortriu">Fortriu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scandinavian-influenced_territories">Scandinavian-influenced territories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Scandinavian-influenced territories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_the_Isles">Kingdom of the Isles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Kingdom of the Isles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_the_Isles" title="Kingdom of the Isles">Kingdom of the Isles</a></div>\n<p>The Kingdom of the Isles comprised the Hebrides, the <a href="/wiki/Islands_of_the_Clyde" title="Islands of the Clyde">islands of the Firth of Clyde</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> from the 9th to the 13th centuries AD. The islands were known to the Norse as the <i>Su\xc3\xb0reyjar</i>, or "Southern Isles" as distinct from the <i>Nor\xc3\xb0reyjar</i> or "<a href="/wiki/Northern_Isles" title="Northern Isles">Northern Isles</a>" of <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a>, which were held by the <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Orkney" title="Earl of Orkney">Earls of Orkney</a> as vassals of the Norwegian crown throughout the High Middle Ages.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg/220px-Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg.png" width="220" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg/330px-Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg/440px-Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1297" data-file-height="1362" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The various kingdoms that comprised Scotland circa 1100</div></div></div>\n<p>After Ragnall ua \xc3\x8dmair, <a href="/wiki/Amla%C3%ADb_Cuar%C3%A1n" title="Amla\xc3\xadb Cuar\xc3\xa1n">Amla\xc3\xadb Cuar\xc3\xa1n</a>, who fought at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Brunanburh" title="Battle of Brunanburh">Battle of Brunanburh</a> in 937 and who also became <a href="/wiki/King_of_Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="King of Northumbria">King of Northumbria</a>, is the next King of the Isles on record.<sup id="cite_ref-G4-6_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G4-6-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> In the succeeding years Norse sources also list various rulers such as <a href="/wiki/Gilli_(Hebridean_earl)" title="Gilli (Hebridean earl)">Gilli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sigurd_the_Stout" title="Sigurd the Stout">Sigurd the Stout</a>, <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A5kon_Eiriksson" class="mw-redirect" title="H\xc3\xa5kon Eiriksson">H\xc3\xa5kon Eiriksson</a><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Thorfinn_the_Mighty" title="Thorfinn the Mighty">Thorfinn Sigurdsson</a> as rulers over the Hebrides as vassals of the Kings of Norway or Denmark.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Godred_Crovan" title="Godred Crovan">Godred Crovan</a> became the ruler of Dublin and Mann from 1079<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-C51_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-C51-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> and from the early years of the twelfth century the <a href="/wiki/Crovan_dynasty" title="Crovan dynasty">Crovan dynasty</a> asserted themselves and ruled as "Kings of Mann and the Isles" for the next half century. The kingdom was then sundered due to the actions of <a href="/wiki/Somerled" title="Somerled">Somerled</a> whose sons inherited the southern Hebrides while the Manx rulers held on to the "north isles" for another century.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_North">The North</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: The North">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Scandinavian influence in Scotland was probably at its height in the mid eleventh century<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> during the time of Thorfinn Sigurdsson, who attempted to create a single political and ecclesiastical domain stretching from Shetland to Man.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The permanent Scandinavian holdings in Scotland at that time must therefore have been at least a quarter of the land area of modern Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;Note 3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the end of the eleventh century the Norwegian crown had come to accept that Caithness was held by the Earls of Orkney as a fiefdom from the Kings of Scotland although its Norse character was retained throughout the thirteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Raghnall_mac_Gofraidh" class="mw-redirect" title="Raghnall mac Gofraidh">Raghnall mac Gofraidh</a> was granted Caithness after assisting the Scots king in a conflict with <a href="/wiki/Harald_Maddadson" class="mw-redirect" title="Harald Maddadson">Harald Maddadson</a>, an earl of Orkney in the early thirteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;Note 4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the ninth century Orcadian control stretched into Moray, which was a semi-independent kingdom for much of this early period.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The Moray rulers <a href="/wiki/Macbeth_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Macbeth of Scotland">MacBeth</a> (1040\xe2\x80\x931057) and his successor <a href="/wiki/Lulach_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Lulach of Scotland">Lulach</a> (1057\xe2\x80\x931058) became rulers of the entire Scottish kingdom for a time.<sup id="cite_ref-Mackie43_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mackie43-48">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> However, Moray was subjugated by the Scottish kings after 1130, when the native ruler, <a href="/wiki/%C3%93engus_of_Moray" title="\xc3\x93engus of Moray">\xc3\x93engus of Moray</a> was killed leading a rebellion. Another revolt in 1187 was equally unsuccessful.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_west_Scotland">South west Scotland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: South west Scotland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:208px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Magnus_III_Barefoot_of_Norway_coin_1865.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a8/Magnus_III_Barefoot_of_Norway_coin_1865.png" width="206" height="207" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="206" data-file-height="207" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Magnus_III_Barefoot_of_Norway_coin_1865.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A coin showing <a href="/wiki/Magnus_Barefoot" title="Magnus Barefoot">Magnus Barefoot</a>, King of Norway (1093\xe2\x80\x931103)</div></div></div>\n<p>By the mid tenth century Amla\xc3\xadb Cuar\xc3\xa1n controlled <a href="/wiki/Wigtownshire" title="Wigtownshire">The Rhinns</a><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> and the region gets the modern name of Galloway from the mixture of Viking and Gaelic Irish settlement that produced the Gall-Gaidel.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Magnus_III_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnus III of Norway">Magnus Barelegs</a> is said to have "subdued the people of Galloway"<sup id="cite_ref-GCB106_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCB106-52">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> in the eleventh century and <a href="/wiki/Whithorn" title="Whithorn">Whithorn</a> seems to have been a centre of Hiberno-Norse artisans who traded around the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Sea" title="Irish Sea">Irish Sea</a> by the end of the first millennium.<sup id="cite_ref-GCB203_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCB203-53">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> However, the place name, written and archaeological evidence of extensive Norse (as opposed to <a href="/wiki/Norse-Gaels" class="mw-redirect" title="Norse-Gaels">Norse-Gael</a>) settlement in the area is not convincing.<sup id="cite_ref-GCB106_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCB106-52">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The ounceland system seems to have become widespread down the west coast including much of Argyll, and most of the south west apart from a region near the inner <a href="/wiki/Solway_Firth" title="Solway Firth">Solway Firth</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Dumfries_and_Galloway" title="Dumfries and Galloway">Dumfries and Galloway</a> the place name evidence is complex and of mixed Gaelic, Norse and <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a> influence, the last most likely stemming from contact with the extensive Danish holdings in northern England.<sup id="cite_ref-C87_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-C87-54">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Although the Scots obtained greater control after the death of <a href="/wiki/Gille_Brigte,_Lord_of_Galloway" class="mw-redirect" title="Gille Brigte, Lord of Galloway">Gilla Brigte</a> and the accession of <a href="/wiki/Lochlann,_Lord_of_Galloway" class="mw-redirect" title="Lochlann, Lord of Galloway">Lochlann</a> in 1185, Galloway was not fully absorbed by Scotland until 1235, after the <a href="/wiki/Gilla_Ruadh_the_Galwegian" class="mw-redirect" title="Gilla Ruadh the Galwegian">rebellion of the Galwegians</a> was crushed.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Strathclyde">Strathclyde</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Strathclyde">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Strathclyde" title="Kingdom of Strathclyde">Kingdom of Strathclyde</a></div>\n<p>The main language of Strathclyde and elsewhere in the <i><a href="/wiki/Hen_Ogledd" title="Hen Ogledd">Hen Ogledd</a></i> in the opening years of the High Middle Ages was <a href="/wiki/Cumbric_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Cumbric language">Cumbric</a>, a variety of the <a href="/wiki/Brythonic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Brythonic languages">British language</a> akin to <a href="/wiki/Old_Welsh" title="Old Welsh">Old Welsh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Some time after 1018 and before 1054, the kingdom appears to have been conquered by the Scots, most probably during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_II_of_Scotland" title="Malcolm II of Scotland">M\xc3\xa1el Coluim mac Cin\xc3\xa1eda</a> who died in 1034. At this time the territory of Strathclyde extended as far south as the <a href="/wiki/River_Derwent,_Cumbria" title="River Derwent, Cumbria">River Derwent</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Price121_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Price121-57">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> In 1054, the English king <a href="/wiki/Edward_the_Confessor" title="Edward the Confessor">Edward the Confessor</a> dispatched Earl <a href="/wiki/Siward_of_Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Siward of Northumbria">Siward of Northumbria</a> against the Scots, then ruled by <a href="/wiki/Macbeth_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Macbeth of Scotland">Mac Beth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> By the 1070s, if not earlier in the reign of <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_III_of_Scotland" title="Malcolm III of Scotland">M\xc3\xa1el Coluim mac Donnchada</a>, it appears that the Scots again controlled Strathclyde, although <a href="/wiki/William_Rufus" class="mw-redirect" title="William Rufus">William Rufus</a> annexed the southern portion in 1092.<sup id="cite_ref-Price121_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Price121-57">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The territory was granted by Alexander I to his brother David, later <a href="/wiki/David_I_of_Scotland" title="David I of Scotland">King David I</a>, in 1107.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_Alba_or_Scotia">Kingdom of Alba or Scotia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Kingdom of Alba or Scotia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Alba" title="Kingdom of Alba">Kingdom of Alba</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gaelic_kings:_Domnall_II_to_Alexander_I">Gaelic kings: Domnall II to Alexander I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Gaelic kings: Domnall II to Alexander I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pictish_Beast.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Pictish_Beast.svg/220px-Pictish_Beast.svg.png" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Pictish_Beast.svg/330px-Pictish_Beast.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Pictish_Beast.svg/440px-Pictish_Beast.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pictish_Beast.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Pictish_Beast" title="Pictish Beast">Pictish Beast</a></i>, by far the most common animal depiction on <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Pictish</a> stones, with unclear political or mythological meaning<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Domnall mac Causant\xc3\xadn\'s nickname was <i>d\xc3\xa1sachtach</i>. This simply meant a madman, or, in early Irish law, a man not in control of his functions and hence without legal culpability.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The following long reign (900\xe2\x80\x93942/3) of his successor <a href="/wiki/Constantine_II_of_Scotland" title="Constantine II of Scotland">Causant\xc3\xadn</a> is more often regarded as the key to formation of the Kingdom of Alba.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The period between the accession of <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_I_of_Scotland" title="Malcolm I of Scotland">M\xc3\xa1el Coluim I</a> and M\xc3\xa1el Coluim mac Cin\xc3\xa1eda was marked by good relations with the <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a> <a href="/wiki/Kings_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Kings of England">rulers of England</a>, intense internal dynastic disunity and, despite this, relatively successful expansionary policies. In 945, king M\xc3\xa1el Coluim I received <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Strathclyde" title="Kingdom of Strathclyde">Strathclyde</a> as part of a deal with King <a href="/wiki/Edmund_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Edmund I of England">Edmund of England</a>, an event offset somewhat by M\xc3\xa1el Coluim\'s loss of control in Moray.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Sometime in the reign of king <a href="/wiki/Indulf_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Indulf of Scotland">Idulb</a> (954\xe2\x80\x93962), the Scots captured the fortress called <i>oppidum Eden</i>, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Scottish control of <a href="/wiki/Lothian" title="Lothian">Lothian</a> was strengthened with M\xc3\xa1el Coluim II\'s victory over the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Northumbria" title="Kingdom of Northumbria">Northumbrians</a> at the Battle of Carham (1018). The Scots had probably some authority in Strathclyde since the later part of the ninth century, but the kingdom kept its own rulers, and it is not clear that the Scots were always strong enough to enforce their authority.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The reign of King <a href="/wiki/Duncan_I_of_Scotland" title="Duncan I of Scotland">Donnchad I</a> from 1034 was marred by failed military adventures, and he was killed in a battle with the men of Moray, led by <a href="/wiki/Macbeth,_King_of_Scotland" title="Macbeth, King of Scotland">MacBeth</a> who became king in 1040.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> MacBeth ruled for seventeen years, so peacefully that he was able to leave to go on <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">pilgrimage</a> to <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>. However, he was overthrown by <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_III_of_Scotland" title="Malcolm III of Scotland">M\xc3\xa1el Coluim</a>, the son of Donnchad, who some months later defeated MacBeth\'s stepson and successor Lulach to become king M\xc3\xa1el Coluim III.<sup id="cite_ref-Mackie43_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mackie43-48">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In subsequent medieval <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a> Donnchad\'s reign was portrayed positively, while MacBeth was vilified; <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a> followed this distorted history in describing both men and the <a href="/wiki/Queen_consort" title="Queen consort">Queen consort</a> <a href="/wiki/Gruoch_of_Scotland" title="Gruoch of Scotland">Gruoch</a> in his play <i><a href="/wiki/Macbeth" title="Macbeth">Macbeth</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;Note 5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Saint_Margaret_of_Scotland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Saint_Margaret_of_Scotland.jpg/220px-Saint_Margaret_of_Scotland.jpg" width="220" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3d/Saint_Margaret_of_Scotland.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="268" data-file-height="252" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Saint_Margaret_of_Scotland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/St_Margaret_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="St Margaret of Scotland">St Margaret of Scotland</a>, wife of <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A1el_Coluim_III_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="M\xc3\xa1el Coluim III of Scotland">M\xc3\xa1el Coluim III</a>, from a later genealogy</div></div></div>\n<p>It was M\xc3\xa1el Coluim III, not his father Donnchad, who did more to create the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Dunkeld" title="House of Dunkeld">dynasty</a> that ruled Scotland for the following two centuries. Part of the resource was the large number of children he had, perhaps as many as a dozen, through marriage to the widow or daughter of Thorfinn Sigurdsson and afterwards to the Anglo-Hungarian princess <a href="/wiki/Saint_Margaret_of_Scotland" title="Saint Margaret of Scotland">Margaret</a>, granddaughter of <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Ironside" title="Edmund Ironside">Edmund Ironside</a>. However, despite having a royal <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxon</a> wife, M\xc3\xa1el Coluim spent much of his reign conducting <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slave</a> raids against the English, adding to the woes of that people in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Norman_Conquest_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman Conquest of England">Norman Conquest of England</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Harrying_of_the_North" title="Harrying of the North">Harrying of the North</a>. <a href="/wiki/Marianus_Scotus" class="mw-redirect" title="Marianus Scotus">Marianus Scotus</a> narrates that "the Gaels and French devastated the English; and [the English] were dispersed and died of hunger; and were compelled to eat human flesh".<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>M\xc3\xa1el Coluim\'s Queen Margaret was the sister of the native claimant to the English throne, <a href="/wiki/Edgar_the_%C3%86theling" class="mw-redirect" title="Edgar the \xc3\x86theling">Edgar \xc3\x86theling</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> This marriage, and M\xc3\xa1el Coluim\'s raids on northern England, prompted interference by the Norman rulers of England in the Scottish kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> King <a href="/wiki/William_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="William I of England">William the Conqueror</a> invaded and M\xc3\xa1el Coluim submitted to his authority, giving his oldest son Donnchad as a hostage.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> From 1079 onwards there were various cross border raids by both parties and M\xc3\xa1el Coluim himself and Edward, his eldest son by Margaret, died in one of them in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Alnwick_(1093)" title="Battle of Alnwick (1093)">Battle of Alnwick</a>, in 1093.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tradition would have made his brother <a href="/wiki/Donald_III_of_Scotland" title="Donald III of Scotland">Domnall B\xc3\xa1n</a> M\xc3\xa1el Coluim\'s successor, but it seems that Edward, his eldest son by Margaret, was his chosen heir. With M\xc3\xa1el Coluim and Edward dead in the same battle, and his other sons in Scotland still young, Domnall was made king. However, <a href="/wiki/Duncan_II_of_Scotland" title="Duncan II of Scotland">Donnchad II</a>, M\xc3\xa1el Coluim\'s eldest son by his first wife, obtained some support from <a href="/wiki/William_II_of_England" title="William II of England">William Rufus</a> and took the throne. According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle" title="Anglo-Saxon Chronicle">Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</a></i> his English and French followers were massacred,<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> and Donnchad II himself was killed later in the same year (1094) by Domnall\'s ally <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A1el_Petair_of_Mearns" title="M\xc3\xa1el Petair of Mearns">M\xc3\xa1el Petair of Mearns</a>. In 1097, William Rufus sent another of M\xc3\xa1el Coluim\'s sons, <a href="/wiki/Edgar_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Edgar of Scotland">Edgar</a>, to take the kingship. The ensuing death of Domnall B\xc3\xa1n secured the kingship for Edgar, and there followed a period of relative peace. The reigns of both Edgar and his successor <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Scotland" title="Alexander I of Scotland">Alexander</a> are obscure by comparison with their successors. The former\'s most notable act was to send a <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">camel</a> (or perhaps an <a href="/wiki/Elephant" title="Elephant">elephant</a>) to his fellow Gael <a href="/wiki/Muircheartach_Ua_Briain" class="mw-redirect" title="Muircheartach Ua Briain">Muircheartach Ua Briain</a>, <a href="/wiki/High_King_of_Ireland" title="High King of Ireland">High King of Ireland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> When Edgar died, Alexander took the kingship, while his youngest brother David became <a href="/wiki/David,_Prince_of_the_Cumbrians" title="David, Prince of the Cumbrians">Prince of Cumbria</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scoto-Norman_kings:_David_I_to_Alexander_III">Scoto-Norman kings: David I to Alexander III</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Scoto-Norman kings: David I to Alexander III">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DavidIofScotland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/30/DavidIofScotland.jpg" width="150" height="150" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="150" data-file-height="150" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DavidIofScotland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Image of <a href="/wiki/David_I_of_Scotland" title="David I of Scotland">David I</a>, a pious and revolutionary Scoto-Norman king</div></div></div>\n<p>The period between the accession of <a href="/wiki/David_I_of_Scotland" title="David I of Scotland">David I</a> and the death of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_III_of_Scotland" title="Alexander III of Scotland">Alexander III</a> was marked by dependency upon and relatively good relations with the Kings of England. The period can be regarded as one of great historical transformation, part of a more general phenomenon, which has been called "Europeanisation".<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The period also witnessed the successful imposition of royal authority across most of the modern country. After David I, and especially in the reign of <a href="/wiki/William_I_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="William I of Scotland">William I</a>, Scotland\'s Kings became ambivalent about the culture of most of their subjects.<sup id="cite_ref-williamcitation_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-williamcitation-76">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Walter_of_Coventry" title="Walter of Coventry">Walter of Coventry</a> tells us, "The modern kings of Scotland count themselves as Frenchmen, in race, manners, language and culture; they keep only Frenchmen in their household and following, and have reduced the Gaels to utter servitude."<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>This situation was not without consequence. In the aftermath of William\'s capture at <a href="/wiki/Alnwick" title="Alnwick">Alnwick</a> in 1174, the Scots turned on the small number of <a href="/wiki/Middle_English_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle English language">Middle English-speakers</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French-speakers</a> among them. <a href="/wiki/William_of_Newburgh" title="William of Newburgh">William of Newburgh</a> related that the Scots first attacked the Scoto-English in their own army, and Newburgh reported a repetition of these events in Scotland itself.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Walter_Bower" title="Walter Bower">Walter Bower</a>, writing a few centuries later about the same events confirms that "there took place a most wretched and widespread persecution of the English both in Scotland and Galloway".<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WilliamI.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8b/WilliamI.jpg" width="150" height="150" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="150" data-file-height="150" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WilliamI.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The seal of <a href="/wiki/William_I_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="William I of Scotland">William I</a>, known as <i>Guillaume le Lion</i>. His title among the <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">native Scots</a> was probably <i>Uilleam Garbh</i> (i.e. "William the Rough").<sup id="cite_ref-williamcitation_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-williamcitation-76">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The first instance of strong opposition to the Scottish kings was perhaps the revolt of <a href="/wiki/%C3%93engus_of_Moray" title="\xc3\x93engus of Moray">\xc3\x93engus</a>, the Mormaer of Moray. Other important resistors to the expansionary Scottish kings were Somerled, <a href="/wiki/Fergus_of_Galloway" title="Fergus of Galloway">Fergus of Galloway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gille_Brigte,_Lord_of_Galloway" class="mw-redirect" title="Gille Brigte, Lord of Galloway">Gille Brigte, Lord of Galloway</a> and Harald Maddadsson, along with two kin-groups known today as the <a href="/wiki/MacHeths" title="MacHeths">MacHeths</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Meic_Uilleim" title="Meic Uilleim">MacWilliams</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The threat from the latter was so grave that, after their defeat in 1230, the Scottish crown ordered the public execution of the infant girl who happened to be the last of the MacWilliam line. According to the <i>Lanercost Chronicle</i>:\n</p>\n<table class="cquote pullquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">\xe2\x80\x9c\n</td>\n<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">the same Mac-William\'s daughter, who had not long left her mother\'s womb, innocent as she was, was put to death, in the burgh of Forfar, in view of the market place, after a proclamation by the public crier. Her head was struck against the column of the market cross, and her brains dashed out.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">\xe2\x80\x9d\n</td></tr>\n\n</tbody></table>\n<p>Many of these resistors collaborated, and drew support not just in the peripheral Gaelic regions of Galloway, Moray, Ross and Argyll, but also from eastern "Scotland-proper", and elsewhere in the Gaelic world. However, by the end of the twelfth century, the Scottish kings had acquired the authority and ability to draw in native Gaelic lords outside their previous zone of control in order to do their work, the most famous examples being <a href="/wiki/Lochlann,_Lord_of_Galloway" class="mw-redirect" title="Lochlann, Lord of Galloway">Lochlann, Lord of Galloway</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fearchar,_Earl_of_Ross" title="Fearchar, Earl of Ross">Ferchar mac in tSagairt</a>. By the reign of Alexander III, the Scots were in a strong position to annex the remainder of the western seaboard, which they did following <a href="/wiki/Haakon_IV_of_Norway" title="Haakon IV of Norway">Haakon Haakonarson</a>\'s ill-fated invasion and the stalemate of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Largs" title="Battle of Largs">Battle of Largs</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Perth" title="Treaty of Perth">Treaty of Perth</a> in 1266.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrett_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrett-82">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The conquest of the west, the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Carrick" title="Earl of Carrick">Mormaerdom of Carrick</a> in 1186 and the absorption of the <a href="/wiki/Lords_of_Galloway" class="mw-redirect" title="Lords of Galloway">Lordship of Galloway</a> after the Galwegian revolt of <a href="/wiki/Gille_Ruadh" title="Gille Ruadh">Gille Ruadh</a> in 1235<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> meant that Gaelic speakers under the rule of the Scottish king formed a majority of the population during the so-called Norman period. The integration of Gaelic, Norman and Saxon culture that began to occur<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> may have been the platform that enabled King <a href="/wiki/Robert_I_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert I of Scotland">Robert I</a> to emerge victorious during the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Wars_of_Independence" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Wars of Independence">Wars of Independence</a>, which followed soon after the death of Alexander III.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Geography of Scotland in the Middle Ages">Geography of Scotland in the Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scotland_grevskap.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Scotland_grevskap.png/170px-Scotland_grevskap.png" width="170" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Scotland_grevskap.png/255px-Scotland_grevskap.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Scotland_grevskap.png/340px-Scotland_grevskap.png 2x" data-file-width="843" data-file-height="1031" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scotland_grevskap.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of <a href="/wiki/Mormaer" title="Mormaer">comital</a> and other Lordships in Medieval Scotland, c. 1230</div></div></div>\n<p>At the beginning of this period the boundaries of Alba contained only a small proportion of modern Scotland. Even when these lands were added to in the tenth and eleventh centuries, the term <i><a href="/wiki/Scotia" title="Scotia">Scotia</a></i> was applied in sources only to the region between the <a href="/wiki/River_Forth" title="River Forth">River Forth</a>, the central <a href="/wiki/Grampian_Mountains" title="Grampian Mountains">Grampians</a> and the <a href="/wiki/River_Spey" title="River Spey">River Spey</a> and only began to be used to describe all of the lands under the authority of the Scottish crown from the second half of the twelfth century.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> By the late thirteenth century when the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_York" title="Treaty of York">Treaty of York</a> (1237) and <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Perth" title="Treaty of Perth">Treaty of Perth</a> (1266) had fixed the boundaries with the Kingdom of the Scots with England and <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> respectively, its borders were close to the modern boundaries. After this time both <a href="/wiki/Berwick-upon-Tweed" title="Berwick-upon-Tweed">Berwick</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> were lost to England, and Orkney and Shetland were gained from Norway in the fifteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The area that became Scotland in this period is divided by geology into five major regions: the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Uplands" title="Southern Uplands">Southern Uplands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Lowlands" title="Central Lowlands">Central Lowlands</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Highlands" title="Scottish Highlands">Highlands</a>, the North-east coastal plain and the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Islands">Islands</a>. Some of these were further divided by mountains, major rivers and marshes. Most of these regions had strong cultural and economic ties elsewhere: to England, Ireland, Scandinavian and mainland Europe. Internal communications were difficult and the country lacked an obvious geographical centre.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dunfermline" title="Dunfermline">Dunfermline</a> emerged as a major royal centre in the reign of Malcolm III and Edinburgh began to be used to house royal records in the reign of David I, but, perhaps because of its proximity and vulnerability to England, it did not become a formal capital in this period.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nThe expansion of Alba into the wider <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Kingdom of Scotland</a> was a gradual process combining external conquest and the suppression of occasional rebellions with the extension of seigniorial power through the placement of effective agents of the crown.<sup id="cite_ref-Grant_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant-90">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Neighbouring independent kings became subject to Alba and eventually disappeared from the records. In the ninth century the term <i><a href="/wiki/Mormaer" title="Mormaer">mormaer</a></i>, meaning "great steward", began to appear in the records to describe the rulers of Moray, <a href="/wiki/Strathearn" title="Strathearn">Strathearn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buchan" title="Buchan">Buchan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angus,_Scotland" title="Angus, Scotland">Angus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kincardineshire" title="Kincardineshire">Mearns</a>, who may have acted as "marcher lords" for the kingdom to counter the Viking threat.<sup id="cite_ref-Webster1997p22_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster1997p22-91">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Later the process of consolidation is associated with the feudalism introduced by David I, which, particularly in the east and south where the crown\'s authority was greatest, saw the placement of lordships, often based on castles, and the creation of administrative <a href="/wiki/Sheriff" title="Sheriff">sheriffdoms</a>, which overlay the pattern of local <a href="/wiki/Thegn" title="Thegn">thegns</a>. It also saw the <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> <i>earl</i> and <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>comes</i> begin to replace the <i>mormaers</i> in the records. The result has been seen as a "hybrid kingdom, in which Gaelic, Anglo-Saxon, Flemish and Norman elements all coalesced under its \'Normanised\', but nevertheless native lines of kings".<sup id="cite_ref-Grant_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant-90">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy_and_society">Economy and society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Economy and society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages" title="Economy of Scotland in the High Middle Ages">Economy of Scotland in the High Middle Ages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Economy of Scotland in the Middle Ages">Economy of Scotland in the Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burghs_of_Dab%C3%ADd_mac_Ma%C3%ADl_Choluim.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Burghs_of_Dab%C3%ADd_mac_Ma%C3%ADl_Choluim.JPG/170px-Burghs_of_Dab%C3%ADd_mac_Ma%C3%ADl_Choluim.JPG" width="170" height="315" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Burghs_of_Dab%C3%ADd_mac_Ma%C3%ADl_Choluim.JPG/255px-Burghs_of_Dab%C3%ADd_mac_Ma%C3%ADl_Choluim.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Burghs_of_Dab%C3%ADd_mac_Ma%C3%ADl_Choluim.JPG/340px-Burghs_of_Dab%C3%ADd_mac_Ma%C3%ADl_Choluim.JPG 2x" data-file-width="599" data-file-height="1111" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burghs_of_Dab%C3%ADd_mac_Ma%C3%ADl_Choluim.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Burghs established in Scotland before the accession of <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_IV_of_Scotland" title="Malcolm IV of Scotland">M\xc3\xa1el Coluim</a>, essentially <a href="/wiki/Scotia" title="Scotia">Scotland-proper\'s</a> first towns</div></div></div>\n<p>The Scottish economy of this period was dominated by agriculture and by short-distance, local trade. There was an increasing amount of foreign trade in the period, as well as exchange gained by means of military plunder. By the end of this period, coins were replacing <a href="/wiki/Barter" title="Barter">barter goods</a>, but for most of this period most exchange was done without the use of metal currency.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most of Scotland\'s agricultural wealth in this period came from <a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">pastoralism</a>, rather than <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable farming</a>. Arable farming grew significantly in the "Norman period", but with geographical differences, low-lying areas being subject to more arable farming than high-lying areas such as the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Highlands" title="Scottish Highlands">Highlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galloway" title="Galloway">Galloway</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Uplands" title="Southern Uplands">Southern Uplands</a>. Galloway, in the words of G. W. S. Barrow, "already famous for its cattle, was so overwhelmingly pastoral, that there is little evidence in that region of land under any permanent cultivation, save along the Solway coast".<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The average amount of land used by a <a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">husbandman</a> in Scotland might have been around 26 <a href="/wiki/Acres" class="mw-redirect" title="Acres">acres</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The native Scots favoured pastoralism, in that Gaelic lords were happier to give away more land to French and Middle English-speaking settlers, while holding on tenaciously to upland regions, perhaps contributing to the Highland/Galloway-Lowland division that emerged in Scotland in the later Middle Ages.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The main unit of land measurement in Scotland was the <i>davoch</i> (i.e. "vat"), called the <i>arachor</i> in <a href="/wiki/Lennox_(district)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lennox (district)">Lennox</a> and also known as the "Scottish ploughgate". In English-speaking Lothian, it was simply <a href="/wiki/Ploughgate" class="mw-redirect" title="Ploughgate">ploughgate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> It may have measured about 104 acres (0.42&#160;km<sup>2</sup>),<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> divided into 4 <i>rath</i>s.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Cattle, pigs and cheeses were among the chief foodstuffs,<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> from a wide range of produce including sheep, fish, rye, barley, bee wax and honey.\n</p><p>David I established the first chartered <a href="/wiki/Burghs" class="mw-redirect" title="Burghs">burghs</a> in Scotland, copying the burgher charters and <i>Leges Burgorum</i> (rules governing virtually every aspect of life and work) almost verbatim from the English customs of <a href="/wiki/Newcastle-Upon-Tyne" class="mw-redirect" title="Newcastle-Upon-Tyne">Newcastle-Upon-Tyne</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Early burgesses were usually <a href="/wiki/Flemings" class="mw-redirect" title="Flemings">Flemish</a>, <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">English</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">German</a>, rather than Gaelic Scots. The burgh\'s vocabulary was composed totally of either Germanic and French terms.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The councils that ran individual burghs were individually known as <i>lie doussane</i>, meaning the dozen.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demography_and_language">Demography and language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Demography and language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Demography_of_Scotland" title="Demography of Scotland">Demography of Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic languages</a></div>\n<p>The population of Scotland in this period is unknown. The first reliable information in 1755 shows the inhabitants of Scotland as 1,265,380. Best estimates put the Scottish population for earlier periods in the High Middle Ages between 500,000 and 1,000,000 people, growing from a low point to a high point.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\nLinguistically, the majority of people within Scotland throughout this period spoke the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Irish_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Irish language">Gaelic</a> language, then simply called <i>Scottish</i>, or in Latin, <i>lingua Scotica</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Other languages spoken throughout this period were <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> and English, with the <a href="/wiki/Cumbric_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Cumbric language">Cumbric language</a> disappearing somewhere between 900 and 1100.<sup id="cite_ref-Price121_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Price121-57">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Pictish may have survived into this period, but the evidence is weak. After the accession of David I, or perhaps before, Gaelic ceased to be the main language of the royal court. From his reign until the end of the period, the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_monarchs" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish monarchs">Scottish monarchs</a> probably favoured the French language, as evidenced by reports from contemporary chronicles, literature and translations of administrative documents into the French language. English, with French and Flemish, became the main language of Scottish burghs. However, they were, in Barrow\'s words, "scarcely more than villages ... numbered in hundreds rather than thousands".<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Society">Society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages" title="Society of Scotland in the High Middle Ages">Society of Scotland in the High Middle Ages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scottish_society_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Scottish society in the Middle Ages">Scottish society in the Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:30%;&#32;; background-color: #E6E6FA;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9c ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9d ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style=""><center><b>Pre-Norman Gaelic Society</b><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup></center>\n<p>This is a rough model based on early Gaelic legal texts. The terminology was very different in Scottish Latin sources.\n</p>\n<ul><li><b>Nemed</b> (sacred person, highest rank)\n<ul><li>Ard r\xc3\xad (high king)</li>\n<li>R\xc3\xad ruirech (provincial king)</li>\n<li>R\xc3\xad t\xc3\xbaath (tribal king)</li>\n<li>Flaith (lord)</li></ul></li>\n<li><b>Nemed</b> (non-rulers)\n<ul><li>Clerics</li>\n<li>Fili (poets)</li></ul></li>\n<li><b>D\xc3\xb3ernemid</b> (lit. base Nemed)\n<ul><li>Brithem (tradesman, harpist, etc)</li></ul></li>\n<li><b>Saoirseach</b> (freeman)\n<ul><li>B\xc3\xb3aire (cattle lord)</li>\n<li>\xc3\x93caire (little lord)</li>\n<li>Fer midboth (semi-independent youth)</li>\n<li>Fuidir (semi freeman)</li></ul></li>\n<li><b>Unfree</b>\n<ul><li>Bothach (serf)</li>\n<li>Senchl\xc3\xa9ithe (hereditary serf)</li>\n<li>Mug (slave)</li></ul></li></ul></div>\n</div>\n<p>The legal tract known as <i><a href="/wiki/Laws_of_the_Brets_and_Scots" class="mw-redirect" title="Laws of the Brets and Scots">Laws of the Brets and Scots</a></i>, probably compiled in the reign of David I, underlines the importance of the kin group as entitled to compensation for the killing of individual members. It also lists five grades of man: <i>King</i>, <i>mormaer</i>, <i>to\xc3\xadsech</i>, <i>\xc3\xb3cthigern</i> and <i>neyfs</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Grant1993p42_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant1993p42-107">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The highest rank below the king, the mormaer ("great officer"), were probably about a dozen provincial rulers, later replaced by the English term <a href="/wiki/Earl" title="Earl">earl</a>. Below them the to\xc3\xadsech (leader), appear to have managed areas of the royal demesne, or that of a mormaer or abbot, within which they would have held substantial estates, sometimes described as <a href="/wiki/Shire" title="Shire">shires</a> and the title was probably equivalent to the later <a href="/wiki/Thane" title="Thane">thane</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrow1989pp15-18_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrow1989pp15-18-108">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The lowest free rank mentioned by the <i>Laws of the Brets and Scots</i>, the <i>\xc3\xb3cthigern</i> (literally, <i>little</i> or <i>young lord</i>), is a term the text does not translate into French.<sup id="cite_ref-Grant1993p42_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant1993p42-107">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> There were probably relatively large numbers of free peasant farmers, called husbandmen or bondmen, in the south and north of the country, but fewer in the lands between the Forth and Sutherland until the twelfth century, when landlords began to encourage the formation of such a class through paying better wages and deliberate immigration.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrow1989pp15-18_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrow1989pp15-18-108">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Below the husbandmen a class of free farmers with smaller parcels of land developed, with <a href="/wiki/Cottar" class="mw-redirect" title="Cottar">cottars</a> and grazing tenants (gresemen).<sup id="cite_ref-Barrow1995p586_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrow1995p586-109">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The non-free <i>naviti</i>, <i>neyfs</i> or serfs existed in various forms of service, with terms with their origins in Irish practice, including <i>cumelache</i>, <i>cumherba</i> and <i>scoloc</i> who were tied to a lord\'s estate and unable to leave it without permission, but who records indicate often absconded for better wages or work in other regions or in the developing burghs.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrow1989pp15-18_108-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrow1989pp15-18-108">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The introduction of feudalism from the time of David I, not only introduced sheriffdoms that overlay the pattern of local thanes,<sup id="cite_ref-Grant1993p42_107-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant1993p42-107">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> but also meant that new tenures were held from the king, or a superior lord, in exchange for loyalty and forms of service that were usually military.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrellpp16-19_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrellpp16-19-110">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> However, the imposition of feudalism continued to sit beside existing system of landholding and tenure and it is not clear how this change impacted on the lives of the ordinary free and unfree workers. In places, feudalism may have tied workers more closely to the land, but the predominantly pastoral nature of Scottish agriculture may have made the imposition of a <a href="/wiki/Manorial_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Manorial system">manorial system</a> on the English model impracticable.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrellpp16-19_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrellpp16-19-110">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Obligations appear to have been limited to occasional labour service, seasonal renders of food, hospitality and money rents.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrow1995p586_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrow1995p586-109">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Law_and_government">Law and government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Law and government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Government_in_Medieval_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Government in Medieval Scotland">Government in Medieval Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Legal_institutions_of_Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages" title="Legal institutions of Scotland in the High Middle Ages">Legal institutions of Scotland in the High Middle Ages</a></div>\n<p>Early Gaelic law tracts, first written down in the ninth century, reveal a society highly concerned with kinship, status, honour and the regulation of blood feuds. Scottish <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> began to take shape at the end of the period, assimilating Gaelic and Celtic law with practices from Anglo-Norman England and <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">the Continent</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> In the twelfth century, and certainly in the thirteenth, strong continental legal influences began to have more effect, such as <a href="/wiki/Canon_law_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Canon law (Catholic Church)">Canon law</a> and various Anglo-Norman practices. Pre-fourteenth century <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">law</a> among the <a href="/wiki/Native_Scots" class="mw-redirect" title="Native Scots">native Scots</a> is not always well attested, but extensive knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Brehon_Laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Brehon Laws">early Gaelic Law</a> gives some basis for its reconstruction. In the earliest extant Scottish legal manuscript, there is a document called <i>Leges inter Brettos et Scottos</i>. The document survives in <a href="/wiki/Old_French_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old French language">Old French</a>, and is almost certainly a French translation of an earlier Gaelic document. It retained a vast number of untranslated Gaelic legal terms.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Later medieval legal documents, written both in Latin and <a href="/wiki/Middle_English_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle English language">Middle English</a>, contain more Gaelic legal terms, examples including <i>slains</i> (Old Irish <i>sl\xc3\xa1n</i> or <i>sl\xc3\xa1inte</i>; exemption) and <i>cumherba</i> (Old Irish <i>comarba</i>; ecclesiastic heir).<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A <i>Judex</i> (pl. <i>judices</i>) represents a post-Norman continuity with the ancient Gaelic orders of lawmen called in English today <i>Brehons</i>. Bearers of the office almost always have Gaelic names north of the Forth or in the south-west. <i>Judices</i> were often royal officials who supervised baronial, abbatial and other lower-ranking "courts".<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> However, the main official of law in the post-Davidian Kingdom of the Scots was the <a href="/wiki/Justiciar" title="Justiciar">Justiciar</a> who held courts and reported to the king personally. Normally, there were two Justiciarships, organised by linguistic boundaries: the Justiciar of Scotia and the Justiciar of Lothian. Sometimes Galloway had its own Justiciar too.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_III_and_Ollamh_R%C3%ADgh.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Alexander_III_and_Ollamh_R%C3%ADgh.JPG/220px-Alexander_III_and_Ollamh_R%C3%ADgh.JPG" width="220" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Alexander_III_and_Ollamh_R%C3%ADgh.JPG/330px-Alexander_III_and_Ollamh_R%C3%ADgh.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Alexander_III_and_Ollamh_R%C3%ADgh.JPG/440px-Alexander_III_and_Ollamh_R%C3%ADgh.JPG 2x" data-file-width="887" data-file-height="779" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_III_and_Ollamh_R%C3%ADgh.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coronation of King Alexander on <a href="/wiki/Moot_Hill" class="mw-redirect" title="Moot Hill">Moot Hill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scone,_Perth_and_Kinross" class="mw-redirect" title="Scone, Perth and Kinross">Scone</a>, beside him are the <a href="/wiki/Mormaer_of_Strathearn" class="mw-redirect" title="Mormaer of Strathearn">Mormaers of Strathearn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mormaer_of_Fife" class="mw-redirect" title="Mormaer of Fife">Fife</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The office of Justiciar and Judex were just two ways that Scottish society was governed.
