In the earlier period, the king "delegated" power to hereditary native "officers" such as the Mormaers/Earls and To\xc3\xadsechs/Thanes. It was a <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a> of gift-giving and bardic lawmen. There were also popular courts, the <i>comhdhail</i>, testament to which are dozens of placenames throughout eastern Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> In the Norman period, sheriffdoms and sheriffs and, to a lesser extent, bishops (see <a href="/wiki/Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages#Ecclesia_Scoticana" title="Scotland in the High Middle Ages">below</a>) became increasingly important. The former enabled the King to effectively administer royal demesne land. During David I\'s reign, royal sheriffs had been established in the king\'s core personal territories; namely, in rough chronological order, at <a href="/wiki/Roxburgh" title="Roxburgh">Roxburgh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scone,_Scotland" title="Scone, Scotland">Scone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berwick-upon-Tweed" title="Berwick-upon-Tweed">Berwick-upon-Tweed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stirling" title="Stirling">Stirling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perth,_Scotland" title="Perth, Scotland">Perth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> By the reign of <a href="/wiki/William_I_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="William I of Scotland">William I</a>, there may have been about 30 royal sheriffdoms, including ones at <a href="/wiki/Ayr" title="Ayr">Ayr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dumfries" title="Dumfries">Dumfries</a>, key locations on the borders of Galloway-<a href="/wiki/Carrick,_Scotland" title="Carrick, Scotland">Carrick</a>. As the distribution and number of sheriffdoms expanded, so did royal control. By the end of the thirteenth century, sheriffdoms had been established in westerly locations as far-flung as <a href="/wiki/Wigtown" title="Wigtown">Wigtown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kintyre" title="Kintyre">Kintyre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Skye" class="mw-redirect" title="Isle of Skye">Skye</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lorne,_Argyll_and_Bute" class="mw-redirect" title="Lorne, Argyll and Bute">Lorne</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Through these, the thirteenth-century Scottish king exercised more control over Scotland than any of his later medieval successors. The king himself was itinerant and had no "capital" as such although Scone performed a key function. By ritual tradition, all Scottish kings in this period had to be crowned there by the <a href="/wiki/Mormaer_of_Strathearn" class="mw-redirect" title="Mormaer of Strathearn">Mormaers of Strathearn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mormaer_of_Fife" class="mw-redirect" title="Mormaer of Fife">Fife</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-fifecitation_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fifecitation-119">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Although King David I tried to build up Roxburgh as a capital,<sup id="cite_ref-fifecitation_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fifecitation-119">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> in the twelfth and thirteenth centuries, more charters were issued at Scone than any other location. Other popular locations were nearby Perth, Stirling, <a href="/wiki/Dunfermline" title="Dunfermline">Dunfermline</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In the earliest part of this era, <a href="/wiki/Forres" title="Forres">Forres</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dunkeld" class="mw-redirect" title="Dunkeld">Dunkeld</a> seem to have been the chief royal residences.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Records from the Scandinavian-held lands are much-less well documented by comparison. <a href="/wiki/Udal_law" title="Udal law">Udal law</a> formed the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Legal_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Legal system">legal system</a> and it is known that the Hebrides were taxed using the <a href="/wiki/Ounceland" title="Ounceland">Ounceland</a> measure.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thing_(assembly)" title="Thing (assembly)">Althings</a> were open-air governmental assemblies that met in the presence of the <i><a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Orkney" title="Earl of Orkney">jarl</a></i> and the meetings were open to virtually all free men. At these sessions decisions were made, laws passed and complaints adjudicated.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Examples include <a href="/wiki/Tingwall,_Shetland" title="Tingwall, Shetland">Tingwall</a> and <a href="/wiki/Law_Ting_Holm" title="Law Ting Holm">Law Ting Holm</a> in Shetland, <a href="/wiki/Dingwall" title="Dingwall">Dingwall</a> in Easter Ross, and <a href="/wiki/Tynwald" title="Tynwald">Tynwald</a> on the Isle of Man.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-thing_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thing-125">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Warfare">Warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Warfare_in_Medieval_Scotland" title="Warfare in Medieval Scotland">Warfare in Medieval Scotland</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_warfare">Land warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Land warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DavidI%26squire.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/DavidI%26squire.jpg/170px-DavidI%26squire.jpg" width="170" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3f/DavidI%26squire.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="245" data-file-height="290" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DavidI%26squire.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/David_I_of_Scotland" title="David I of Scotland">David I</a> knighting a squire</div></div></div>\n<p>By the twelfth century the ability of lords and the king to call on wider bodies of men beyond their household troops for major campaigns had become the "common" (<i>communis exertcitus</i>) or "Scottish army" (<i>exercitus Scoticanus</i>), the result of a universal obligation based on the holding of variously named units of land.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Later decrees indicated that the common army was a levy of all able-bodied freemen aged between 16 and 60, with 8-days warning.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-127">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> It produced relatively large numbers of men serving for a limited period, usually as unarmoured or poorly armoured bowmen and spearmen.<sup id="cite_ref-Stringer_1993_pp._30-31_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stringer_1993_pp._30-31-128">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> In this period it continued to be mustered by the <a href="/wiki/Mormaer" title="Mormaer">earls</a> and they often led their men in battle, as was the case in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Standard" title="Battle of the Standard">Battle of the Standard</a> in 1138. It would continue to provide the vast majority of Scottish national armies, potentially producing tens of thousands of men for short periods of conflict, into the early modern era.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There also developed obligations that produced smaller numbers of feudal troops. The Davidian Revolution of the twelfth century was seen by Geoffrey Barrow as bringing "fundamental innovations in military organization". These included the <a href="/wiki/Knight%27s_fee" title="Knight&#39;s fee">knight\'s fee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Homage_(feudal)" title="Homage (feudal)">homage and fealty</a>, as well as castle-building and the regular use of professional cavalry,<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> as knights held castles and estates in exchange for service, providing troops on a 40-day basis.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-127">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> David\'s Norman followers and their retinues were able to provide a force of perhaps 200 mounted and armoured knights, but the vast majority of his forces were the "common army" of poorly armed infantry, capable of performing well in raiding and guerrilla warfare. Although such troops were only infrequently able to stand up to the English in the field, nonetheless they did manage to do so critically in the wars of independence at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stirling_Bridge" title="Battle of Stirling Bridge">Stirling Bridge</a> in 1297 and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bannockburn" title="Battle of Bannockburn">Bannockburn</a> in 1314.<sup id="cite_ref-Stringer_1993_pp._30-31_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stringer_1993_pp._30-31-128">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marine_warfare">Marine warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Marine warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wikingerschiff_Oslo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Wikingerschiff_Oslo.jpg/220px-Wikingerschiff_Oslo.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Wikingerschiff_Oslo.jpg/330px-Wikingerschiff_Oslo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Wikingerschiff_Oslo.jpg/440px-Wikingerschiff_Oslo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wikingerschiff_Oslo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The preserved remains of the <a href="/wiki/Oseberg_ship" class="mw-redirect" title="Oseberg ship">Oseberg ship</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Viking_Ship_Museum_(Oslo)" title="Viking Ship Museum (Oslo)">Viking Ship Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Oslo" title="Oslo">Oslo</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Viking onslaught of the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> was based on superior sea-power, which enabled the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Thalassocracy" title="Thalassocracy">thalassocracies</a> of the north and west. In the late tenth century the naval battle of "Innisibsolian" (tentatively identified as taking place near the <a href="/wiki/Slate_Islands" title="Slate Islands">Slate Islands</a> of Argyll)<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> was won by Alban forces over Vikings, although this was an unusual setback for the Norse. In 962 <a href="/wiki/Indulf" title="Indulf">Ildulb mac Causant\xc3\xadn</a>, King of Scots, was killed (according to the <i><a href="/wiki/Chronicle_of_the_Kings_of_Alba" title="Chronicle of the Kings of Alba">Chronicle of the Kings of Alba</a></i>) fighting the Norse near <a href="/wiki/Cullen,_Moray" title="Cullen, Moray">Cullen</a>, at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bauds" title="Battle of Bauds">Battle of Bauds</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> and although there is no evidence of permanent Viking settlement on the east coast of Scotland south of the Moray Firth, raids and even invasions certainly occurred. <a href="/wiki/Dunnottar_Castle" title="Dunnottar Castle">Dunnottar</a> was taken during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Donald_II_of_Scotland" title="Donald II of Scotland">Domnall mac Causant\xc3\xadn</a><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> and the <i>Orkneyinga saga</i> records an attack on the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_May" title="Isle of May">Isle of May</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Sweyn_Asleifsson" title="Sweyn Asleifsson">Sweyn Asleifsson</a> and Margad Grimsson.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\nThe <a href="/wiki/Viking_ships" title="Viking ships">long-ship</a>, the key to their success, was a graceful, long, narrow, light, wooden boat with a shallow draft hull designed for speed. This shallow draft allowed navigation in waters only one metre deep and permitted beach landings, while its light weight enabled it to be carried over <a href="/wiki/Portage" title="Portage">portages</a>. Longships were also double-ended, the symmetrical bow and stern allowing the ship to reverse direction quickly without having to turn around.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> In the <i><a href="/wiki/G%C3%A0idhealtachd" title="G\xc3\xa0idhealtachd">G\xc3\xa0idhealtachd</a></i> they were eventually succeeded by the <a href="/wiki/Birlinn" title="Birlinn">Birlinn</a>, highland <a href="/wiki/Galley" title="Galley">galley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lymphad" title="Lymphad">lymphad</a>, which, in ascending order of size,<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> and which replaced the steering-board with a stern-rudder from the late twelfth century.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Forces of ships were raised through obligations of a ship-levy through the system of ouncelands and pennylands, which have been argued to date back to the muster system of D\xc3\xa1l Riata, but were probably introduced by Scandinavian settlers.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Later evidence suggests that the supply of ships for war became linked to military <a href="/wiki/Feudal_obligations" class="mw-redirect" title="Feudal obligations">feudal obligations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Viking naval power was disrupted by conflicts between the Scandinavian kingdoms, but entered a period of resurgence in the 13th century when Norwegian kings began to build some of the largest ships seen in Northern European waters, until Haakon Haakonson\'s ill-fated expedition in 1263 left the Scottish crown the most significant power in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-Macquarrie2004p153_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macquarrie2004p153-143">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity_and_the_Church">Christianity and the Church</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Christianity and the Church">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Medieval_Scotland" title="Christianity in Medieval Scotland">Christianity in Medieval Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brecbennoch.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Brecbennoch.PNG/220px-Brecbennoch.PNG" width="220" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Brecbennoch.PNG/330px-Brecbennoch.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Brecbennoch.PNG/440px-Brecbennoch.PNG 2x" data-file-width="786" data-file-height="482" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brecbennoch.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Monymusk_Reliquary" title="Monymusk Reliquary">Monymusk Reliquary</a>, c. 750, thought to be the <i>Brecbennoch</i>, purportedly enclosing the bones of <a href="/wiki/Columba" title="Columba">Columba</a>, and which was carried into the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bannockburn" title="Battle of Bannockburn">Battle of Bannockburn</a> in 1314.<sup id="cite_ref-NMS_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NMS-144">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>By the tenth century all of northern Britain was Christianised, except the Scandinavian north and west, which had been lost to the church in the face of Norse settlement.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Saints">Saints</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Saints">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Like every other Christian country, one of the main features of medieval Scottish Christianity was the <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">Cult of Saints</a>. Saints of Irish origin who were particularly revered included various figures called <a href="/wiki/Fillan" title="Fillan">St Faelan</a> and <a href="/wiki/St._Colman_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Colman (disambiguation)">St. Colman</a>, and saints <a href="/wiki/Finbarr_of_Cork" title="Finbarr of Cork">Findbar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Finan" class="mw-redirect" title="Finan">Finan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> The most important missionary saint was <a href="/wiki/Columba" title="Columba">Columba</a>, who emerged as a national figure in the combined Scottish and Pictish kingdom,<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> with a new centre established in the east at <a href="/wiki/Dunkeld" class="mw-redirect" title="Dunkeld">Dunkeld</a> by Kenneth I for part of his relics. He remained a major figure into the fourteenth century and a new foundation was endowed by William I at <a href="/wiki/Arbroath_Abbey" title="Arbroath Abbey">Arbroath Abbey</a> and the relics in the <a href="/wiki/Monymusk_Reliquary" title="Monymusk Reliquary">Monymusk Reliquary</a> handed over to the Abbot\'s care.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Regional saints remained important to local identities. In Strathclyde the most important saint was <a href="/wiki/St_Kentigern" class="mw-redirect" title="St Kentigern">St Kentigern</a>, in Lothian, <a href="/wiki/St_Cuthbert" class="mw-redirect" title="St Cuthbert">St Cuthbert</a><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> and after this martyrdom around 1115 a cult emerged in Orkney, Shetland and northern Scotland around <a href="/wiki/Magnus_Erlendsson,_Earl_of_Orkney" title="Magnus Erlendsson, Earl of Orkney">Magnus Erlendsson, Earl of Orkney</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> The cult of <a href="/wiki/St_Andrew" class="mw-redirect" title="St Andrew">St Andrew</a> in Scotland was established on the Eastern coast by the Pictish kings as early as the eighth century.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrow_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrow-151">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> The shrine, which from the twelfth century was said to have contained the relics of the saint, brought to Scotland by <a href="/wiki/Saint_Regulus" title="Saint Regulus">Saint Regulus</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> began to attract pilgrims from Scotland, but also from England and further away. By the twelfth century the site at Kilrymont, had become known simply as <a href="/wiki/St._Andrews" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Andrews">St. Andrews</a> and it became increasingly associated with <a href="/wiki/Scottish_national_identity" title="Scottish national identity">Scottish national identity</a> and the royal family.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrow_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrow-151">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> It was renewed as a focus for devotion with the patronage of Queen Margaret,<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> who also became important after her canonisation in 1250 and the ceremonial transfer of her remains to <a href="/wiki/Dunfermline_Abbey" title="Dunfermline Abbey">Dunfermline Abbey</a>, as one of the most revered national saints.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrow_151-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrow-151">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organisation">Organisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Organisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Peter_nicolai_arbo,_olaf_tryggvasson_king.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A group of warriors in medieval garb surround two men whose postures suggest they are about to embrace. The man on the right is taller, has long fair hair and wears a bright red tunic. The man on the left his balding with short grey hair and a white beard. He wears a long brown cloak." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Peter_nicolai_arbo%2C_olaf_tryggvasson_king.jpg/220px-Peter_nicolai_arbo%2C_olaf_tryggvasson_king.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Peter_nicolai_arbo%2C_olaf_tryggvasson_king.jpg/330px-Peter_nicolai_arbo%2C_olaf_tryggvasson_king.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Peter_nicolai_arbo%2C_olaf_tryggvasson_king.jpg/440px-Peter_nicolai_arbo%2C_olaf_tryggvasson_king.jpg 2x" data-file-width="460" data-file-height="294" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Peter_nicolai_arbo,_olaf_tryggvasson_king.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Olaf_I_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Olaf I of Norway">King Olav Tryggvason</a> of Norway, who forcibly Christianised <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-T69_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-T69-154">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Painting by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Nicolai_Arbo" title="Peter Nicolai Arbo">Peter Nicolai Arbo</a></div></div></div>\n<p>There is some evidence that Christianity made inroads into the Viking controlled <a href="/wiki/Highlands_and_Islands" title="Highlands and Islands">Highlands and Islands</a> before the official conversion at the end of the tenth century. There are a relatively large number of isles called Pabbay or Papa in the Western and Northern Isles, which may indicate a "hermit\'s" or "priest\'s isle" from this period. Changes in patterns of grave goods and the use of Viking place names using -kirk also suggest that the Christianity had begun to spread before the official conversion.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> According to the <i>Orkneyinga Saga</i> the Northern Isles were Christianised by <a href="/wiki/Olaf_I_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Olaf I of Norway">Olav Tryggvasson</a> in 995 when he stopped at <a href="/wiki/South_Walls" title="South Walls">South Walls</a> on his way from Ireland to Norway. The King summoned the <i><a href="/wiki/Earl" title="Earl">jarl</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Sigurd_the_Stout" title="Sigurd the Stout">Sigurd the Stout</a> and said "I order you and all your subjects to be baptised. If you refuse, I\'ll have you killed on the spot and I swear I will ravage every island with fire and steel." Unsurprisingly, Sigurd agreed and the islands became Christian at a stroke,<sup id="cite_ref-T69_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-T69-154">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> receiving their own <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Orkney" title="Bishop of Orkney">bishop</a> in the early eleventh century.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;Note 6&#93;</a></sup> Elsewhere in Scandinavian Scotland the record is less clear. There was a <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_the_Isles" title="Bishop of the Isles">Bishop of Iona</a> until the late tenth century and there is then a gap of more than a century, possibly filled by the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Orkney" title="Bishop of Orkney">Bishops of Orkney</a>, before the appointment of the first <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_the_Isles" title="Diocese of the Isles">Bishop of Mann</a> in 1079.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;Note 7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Abernethy_Tower.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Abernethy_Tower.jpg/170px-Abernethy_Tower.jpg" width="170" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Abernethy_Tower.jpg/255px-Abernethy_Tower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Abernethy_Tower.jpg/340px-Abernethy_Tower.jpg 2x" data-file-width="424" data-file-height="598" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Abernethy_Tower.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Abernethy,_Perth_and_Kinross" title="Abernethy, Perth and Kinross">Abernethy</a> round tower, which dates from c. 1100<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>At the beginning of the period Scottish monasticism was dominated by <a href="/wiki/Monks" class="mw-redirect" title="Monks">monks</a> called <i>C\xc3\xa9li D\xc3\xa9</i> (lit. "vassals of God"), anglicised as <a href="/wiki/Culdees" title="Culdees">culdees</a>. At St Andrews and elsewhere, <i>C\xc3\xa9li D\xc3\xa9</i> abbeys are recorded and the round towers at <a href="/wiki/Brechin" title="Brechin">Brechin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abernethy,_Perth_and_Kinross" title="Abernethy, Perth and Kinross">Abernethy</a> are evidence of Irish influence.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Gaelic monasticism was vibrant and expansionary for much of the period and dozens of monasteries, often called <a href="/wiki/Schottenkl%C3%B6ster" class="mw-redirect" title="Schottenkl\xc3\xb6ster">Schottenkl\xc3\xb6ster</a>, were founded by Gaelic monks on the continent.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> The introduction of the continental type of monasticism to Scotland is associated with Queen Margaret, the wife of M\xc3\xa1el Coluim III, although her exact role is unclear. She was in communication with <a href="/wiki/Lanfranc" title="Lanfranc">Lanfranc</a>, Archbishop of Canterbury, and he provided a few monks for a new <a href="/wiki/Benedictine" class="mw-redirect" title="Benedictine">Benedictine</a> <a href="/wiki/Dunfermline_Abbey" title="Dunfermline Abbey">abbey at Dunfermline</a> (c. 1070).<sup id="cite_ref-Macquarrie2004pp117-128_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macquarrie2004pp117-128-165">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Subsequent foundations under Margaret\'s sons, the kings Edgar, Alexander I and particularly David I, tended to be of the reformed type that followed the lead set by <a href="/wiki/Cluniac_Reforms" title="Cluniac Reforms">Cluny</a>. These stressed the original Benedictine virtues of <a href="/wiki/Poverty,_chastity_and_obedience" class="mw-redirect" title="Poverty, chastity and obedience">poverty, chastity and obedience</a>, but also contemplation and service of the Mass and were followed in various forms by large numbers of reformed Benedictine, <a href="/wiki/Augustinian" class="mw-redirect" title="Augustinian">Augustinian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cistercian" class="mw-redirect" title="Cistercian">Cistercian</a> houses.<sup id="cite_ref-Macquarrie2004pp117-128_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macquarrie2004pp117-128-165">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Before the twelfth century most Scottish churches had collegiate bodies of clergy who served over a wide area, often tied together by devotion to a particular missionary saint.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> From this period local lay landholders, perhaps following the example of David I, began to adopt the continental practice of building churches on their land for the local population and endowing them with land and a priest, beginning in the south, spreading to the north-east and then the west, being almost universal by the first survey of the Scottish Church for papal taxation in 1274. The administration of these parishes was often given over to local monastic institutions in a process known as appropriation.<sup id="cite_ref-Macquarrie2004pp117-128_165-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macquarrie2004pp117-128-165">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Scotland had little clear diocesan structure before the Norman period. There were bishoprics based on various ancient churches, but some are very obscure in the records and there appear to be long vacancies.<sup id="cite_ref-Macquarrie2004pp109-117_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macquarrie2004pp109-117-167">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> From around 1070, in the reign of Malcolm III, there was a "Bishop of Alba" resident at St. Andrews, but it is not clear what authority he had over the other bishops. After the <a href="/wiki/Norman_Conquest_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman Conquest of England">Norman Conquest of England</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop" title="Archbishop">Archbishops</a> of both <a href="/wiki/Archbishopric_of_Canterbury" class="mw-redirect" title="Archbishopric of Canterbury">Canterbury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archbishopric_of_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Archbishopric of York">York</a> each claimed superiority over the Scottish church.<sup id="cite_ref-Macquarrie2004pp109-117_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macquarrie2004pp109-117-167">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> The church in Scotland attained independent status after the <a href="/wiki/Papal_Bull" class="mw-redirect" title="Papal Bull">Papal Bull</a> of <a href="/wiki/Celestine_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Celestine III">Celestine III</a> (<i>Cum universi</i>, 1192) by which all Scottish bishoprics except Galloway were formally independent of York and Canterbury. However, unlike Ireland, which had been granted four Archbishoprics in the same century, Scotland received no Archbishop and the whole <i>Ecclesia Scoticana</i>, with individual Scottish bishoprics (except Whithorn/Galloway), became the "special daughter of the see of Rome". It was in practice run by special councils made up of all its bishops, with the bishop of St Andrews emerging as the most important player.<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp26-9_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp26-9-168">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages" title="Culture of Scotland in the High Middle Ages">Culture of Scotland in the High Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BookDeerMattCh1vv18_21Fol05r.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/BookDeerMattCh1vv18_21Fol05r.jpg/170px-BookDeerMattCh1vv18_21Fol05r.jpg" width="170" height="238" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/BookDeerMattCh1vv18_21Fol05r.jpg/255px-BookDeerMattCh1vv18_21Fol05r.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/BookDeerMattCh1vv18_21Fol05r.jpg/340px-BookDeerMattCh1vv18_21Fol05r.jpg 2x" data-file-width="453" data-file-height="633" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BookDeerMattCh1vv18_21Fol05r.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Deer" title="Book of Deer">Book of Deer</a>, Folio 5r contains the text of the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthew" title="Gospel of Matthew">Gospel of Matthew</a> from 1:18 through 1:21. Note the Chi Rho monogram in the upper left corner. The margins contain Gaelic text.</div></div></div>\n<p>As a predominantly Gaelic society, most Scottish cultural practices throughout this period mirrored closely those of <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, or at least those of Ireland with some Pictish borrowings. After David I, the French-speaking kings introduced cultural practices popular in Anglo-Norman England, France and elsewhere. As in all pre-modern societies, storytelling was popular. The English scholar D. D. R. Owen, who specialises in the literature of this era, writes that "Professional storytellers would ply their trade from court to court. Some of them would have been native Scots, no doubt offering legends from the ancient Celtic past performed ... in Gaelic when appropriate, but in French for most of the new nobility".<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Almost all of these stories are lost, although some have come down in the Gaelic or <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots</a> oral tradition. One form of oral culture extremely well accounted for in this period is <a href="/wiki/Genealogy" title="Genealogy">genealogy</a>. There are dozens of Scottish genealogies surviving from this era, covering everyone from the <a href="/wiki/Mormaer_of_Lennox" class="mw-redirect" title="Mormaer of Lennox">Mormaers of Lennox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mormaer_of_Moray" title="Mormaer of Moray">Moray</a> to the Scottish king himself. Scotland\'s kings maintained an <i>ollamh righe</i>, a royal high poet who had a permanent place in all medieval Gaelic lordships, and whose purpose was to recite genealogies when needed, for occasions such as coronations.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Before the reign of David I, the Scots possessed a flourishing literary elite who regularly produced texts in both Gaelic and Latin that were frequently transmitted to Ireland and elsewhere. Dauvit Broun has shown that a Gaelic literary elite survived in the eastern Scottish lowlands, in places such as <a href="/wiki/Loch_Leven_(Kinross)" title="Loch Leven (Kinross)">Loch Leven</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brechin" title="Brechin">Brechin</a> into the thirteenth century,<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> However, surviving records are predominantly written in Latin, and their authors would usually translate vernacular terms into Latin, so that historians are faced with researching a Gaelic society clothed in Latin terminology. Even names were translated into more common continental forms; for instance, <i>Gilla Brigte</i> became <i>Gilbert</i>, <i>\xc3\x81ed</i> became <i>Hugh</i>, etc.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> As far as written literature is concerned, there may be more medieval Scottish Gaelic literature than is often thought. Almost all medieval Gaelic literature has survived because it was sustained in <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, not in Scotland. Thomas Owen Clancy has recently all but proven that the <i><a href="/wiki/Lebor_Bretnach" title="Lebor Bretnach">Lebor Bretnach</a></i>, the so-called "Irish Nennius", was written in Scotland, and probably at the monastery in Abernethy. Yet this text survives only from manuscripts preserved in Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Other literary work that has survived include that of the prolific poet <a href="/wiki/Gille_Brighde_Albanach" class="mw-redirect" title="Gille Brighde Albanach">Gille Brighde Albanach</a>. About 1218, Gille Brighde wrote a poem \xe2\x80\x94 <i>Heading for Damietta</i> \xe2\x80\x94 on his experiences of the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Crusade" title="Fifth Crusade">Fifth Crusade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> In the thirteenth century, <a href="/wiki/Old_French_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old French language">French</a> flourished as a <a href="/wiki/Literary_language" title="Literary language">literary language</a>, and produced the <i><a href="/wiki/Roman_de_Fergus" title="Roman de Fergus">Roman de Fergus</a></i>, one of the earliest pieces of non-Celtic <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a> literature to survive from Scotland.\n</p><p>There is no extant literature in the English language in this era. There is some Norse literature from Scandinavian parts such as <i><a href="/wiki/Darra%C3%B0arlj%C3%B3%C3%B0" title="Darra\xc3\xb0arlj\xc3\xb3\xc3\xb0">Darra\xc3\xb0arlj\xc3\xb3\xc3\xb0</a></i>, which is located in <a href="/wiki/Caithness" title="Caithness">Caithness</a>, the story being a "powerful mixture of Celtic and Old Norse imagery".<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> The famous <i><a href="/wiki/Orkneyinga_Saga" class="mw-redirect" title="Orkneyinga Saga">Orkneyinga Saga</a></i>, which pertains to the early history of the <a href="/wiki/Earldom_of_Orkney" title="Earldom of Orkney">Earldom of Orkney</a>, was written down in <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Celtic_harp_dsc05425.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Celtic_harp_dsc05425.jpg/170px-Celtic_harp_dsc05425.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Celtic_harp_dsc05425.jpg/255px-Celtic_harp_dsc05425.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Celtic_harp_dsc05425.jpg/340px-Celtic_harp_dsc05425.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1944" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Celtic_harp_dsc05425.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of only three surviving medieval Gaelic harps (<a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Museum of Scotland">Museum of Scotland</a>)</div></div></div>\n<p>In the Middle Ages, Scotland was renowned for its musical skill. <a href="/wiki/Gerald_of_Wales" title="Gerald of Wales">Gerald of Wales</a>, a medieval clergyman and chronicler, explains the relation between Scottish and Irish music:\n</p>\n<table class="cquote pullquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">\xe2\x80\x9c\n</td>\n<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">Scotland, because of her affinity and intercourse [with Ireland], tries to imitate Ireland in music and strives in emulation. Ireland uses and delights in two instruments only, the harp namely, and the tympanum. Scotland uses three, the harp, the tympanum and the crowd. In the opinion, however, of many, Scotland has by now not only caught up on Ireland, her instructor, but already far outdistances her and excels her in musical skill. Therefore, [Irish] people now look to that country as the fountain of the art.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">\xe2\x80\x9d\n</td></tr>\n\n</tbody></table><p>\nPlaying the <a href="/wiki/Harp" title="Harp">harp</a> (<i>clarsach</i>) was especially popular with medieval Scots \xe2\x80\x93 half a century after Gerald\'s writing, King Alexander III kept a royal harpist at his court. Of the three medieval harps that survive, two come from Scotland (Perthshire), and one from Ireland. Singers also had a royal function. For instance, when the king of Scotland passed through the territory of Strathearn, it was the custom that he be greeted by seven female singers, who would sing to him. When Edward I approached the borders of Strathearn in the summer of 1296, he was met by these seven women, "who accompanied the King on the road between <a href="/wiki/Gask" title="Gask">Gask</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ogilvie,_Angus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ogilvie, Angus (page does not exist)">Ogilvie</a>, singing to him, as was the custom in the time of the late Alexander kings of Scots".<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span id="Outsiders.27_views"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Outsiders\'_views">Outsiders\' views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Outsiders&#039; views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1138_Scottish_Atrocities_trimmed.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/1138_Scottish_Atrocities_trimmed.JPG/170px-1138_Scottish_Atrocities_trimmed.JPG" width="170" height="261" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ed/1138_Scottish_Atrocities_trimmed.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="242" data-file-height="371" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1138_Scottish_Atrocities_trimmed.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scottish atrocities depicted in the fourteenth century <a href="/wiki/Luttrell_Psalter" title="Luttrell Psalter">Luttrell Psalter</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Irish thought of Scotland as a provincial place. Others thought of it as an outlandish or barbaric place. "Who would deny that the Scots are barbarians?" was a rhetorical question posed by the author of the <i>De expugnatione Lyxbonensi</i> (i.e. "On the Conquest of Lisbon").<sup id="cite_ref-MacQ15_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacQ15-178">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> A century later <a href="/wiki/Louis_IX" class="mw-redirect" title="Louis IX">Louis IX</a> of France was reported to have said to his son "I would prefer that a Scot should come from Scotland and govern the people well and faithfully, than that you, my son, should be seen to govern badly."<sup id="cite_ref-MacQ15_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacQ15-178">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>This characterisation of the Scots was often politically motivated, and many of the most hostile writers were based in areas frequently subjected to Scottish raids. English and French accounts of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Standard" title="Battle of the Standard">Battle of the Standard</a> contain many accounts of Scottish atrocities. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Henry_of_Huntingdon" title="Henry of Huntingdon">Henry of Huntingdon</a> notes that the Scots: "cleft open pregnant women, and took out the unborn babes; they tossed children upon the spear-points, and beheaded priests on altars: they cut the head of crucifixes, and placed them on the trunks of the slain; and placed the heads of the dead upon the crucifixes. Thus wherever the Scots arrived, all was full of horror and full of savagery."<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> A less hostile view was given by <a href="/wiki/Guibert_of_Nogent" title="Guibert of Nogent">Guibert of Nogent</a> in the <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a>, who encountered Scots and wrote that: "You might have seen a crowd of Scots, a people savage at home but unwarlike elsewhere, descend from their marshy lands, with bare legs, shaggy cloaks, their purse hanging from their shoulders; their copious arms seemed ridiculous to us, but they offered their faith and devotion as aid."<sup id="cite_ref-MacQ15_178-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacQ15-178">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There was also a general belief that Scotland-proper was an island, or at least a <a href="/wiki/Peninsula" title="Peninsula">peninsula</a>, known as Scotia, Alba or Albania. <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Paris" title="Matthew Paris">Matthew Paris</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Benedictine" class="mw-redirect" title="Benedictine">Benedictine</a> monk and cartographer, drew a map in this manner in the mid-thirteenth century and called the "island" <i>Scotia ultra marina</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> A later medieval Italian map applies this geographical conceptualization to all of Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> The Arab geographer <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Idrisi" title="Muhammad al-Idrisi">al-Idrisi</a>, shared this view: "Scotland adjoins the island of England and is a long peninsula to the north of the larger island. It is uninhabited and has neither town nor village. Its length is 150 miles."<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="National_identity">National identity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: National identity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scottish_national_identity" title="Scottish national identity">Scottish national identity</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Banner_of_Scotland.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Royal_Banner_of_Scotland.svg/220px-Royal_Banner_of_Scotland.svg.png" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Royal_Banner_of_Scotland.svg/330px-Royal_Banner_of_Scotland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Royal_Banner_of_Scotland.svg/440px-Royal_Banner_of_Scotland.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="375" data-file-height="250" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Banner_of_Scotland.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Royal Standard of Scotland, first adopted by king <a href="/wiki/William_I_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="William I of Scotland">William I</a>, (1143\xe2\x80\x931214)</div></div></div>\n<p>In this period, the word "Scot" was not the word used by vast majority of Scots to describe themselves, except to foreigners, among whom it was the most common word. The Scots called themselves <i>Albanach</i> or simply <i>Gaidel</i>. Both "Scot" and <i>Gaidel</i> were ethnic terms that connected them to the majority of the inhabitants of Ireland. As the author of <i><a href="/wiki/De_Situ_Albanie" title="De Situ Albanie">De Situ Albanie</a></i> notes at the beginning of the thirteenth century: "The name Arregathel [Argyll] means margin of the Scots or Irish, because all Scots and Irish are generally called \'Gattheli\'."<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Likewise, the inhabitants of English and Norse-speaking parts were ethnically linked with other regions of Europe. At <a href="/wiki/Melrose,_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Melrose, Scotland">Melrose</a>, people could recite religious literature in the English language.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> In the later part of the twelfth century, the Lothian writer <a href="/wiki/Adam_of_Dryburgh" title="Adam of Dryburgh">Adam of Dryburgh</a> describes Lothian as "the Land of the English in the Kingdom of the Scots".<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> In the Northern Isles the Norse language evolved into the local <a href="/wiki/Norn_language" title="Norn language">Norn</a>, which lingered until the end of the eighteenth century, when it finally died out<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> and Norse may also have survived as a spoken language until the sixteenth century in the <a href="/wiki/Outer_Hebrides" title="Outer Hebrides">Outer Hebrides</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Scotland came to possess a unity that transcended Gaelic, English, Norman and Norse ethnic differences and by the end of the period, the Latin, Norman-French and English word "Scot" could be used for any subject of the Scottish king. Scotland\'s multilingual <a href="/wiki/Scoto-Norman" title="Scoto-Norman">Scoto-Norman</a> monarchs and mixed Gaelic and Scoto-Norman aristocracy all became part of the "Community of the Realm", in which ethnic differences were less divisive than in Ireland and Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Amla%C3%ADb_Conung" title="Amla\xc3\xadb Conung">Amla\xc3\xadb</a> and his brother Auisle "ravaged the whole of Pictland and took their hostages" and later occupied this territory for a protracted period.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> The earliest versions include the <i><a href="/wiki/Life_of_St_Cathr%C3%B3e_of_Metz" class="mw-redirect" title="Life of St Cathr\xc3\xb3e of Metz">Life of St Cathr\xc3\xb3e of Metz</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> and royal <a href="/wiki/Genealogy" title="Genealogy">genealogies</a> tracing their origin to <a href="/wiki/Fergus_M%C3%B3r_mac_Eirc" class="mw-redirect" title="Fergus M\xc3\xb3r mac Eirc">Fergus M\xc3\xb3r mac Eirc</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In the reign of <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_III_of_Scotland" title="Malcolm III of Scotland">M\xc3\xa1el Coluim III</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Duan_Albanach" title="Duan Albanach">Duan Albanach</a></i> formalised the myth in Gaelic poetic tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In the 13th and 14th centuries, these mythical traditions were incorporated into the documents now in the <a href="/wiki/Poppleton_Manuscript" class="mw-redirect" title="Poppleton Manuscript">Poppleton Manuscript</a>, and in the <i><a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_Arbroath" title="Declaration of Arbroath">Declaration of Arbroath</a></i>. They were considered authentic in the early modern period and beyond; even King <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="James I of England">James VI/I</a> traced his origin to Fergus, saying, in his own words, that he was a "Monarch sprunge of Ferguse race".<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The land areas of the <i>Su\xc3\xb0reyjar</i> are as follows: the Isle of Man is 572 square kilometres (221&#160;sq&#160;mi);<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> the Islands of the Clyde 574 square kilometres (222&#160;sq&#160;mi);<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> the Inner Hebrides 4,158 square kilometres (1,605&#160;sq&#160;mi);<sup id="cite_ref-GRO_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GRO-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> the Outer Hebrides 3,070 square kilometres (1,185&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Caithness and Sutherland have a combined area of 7,051 square kilometres (2,722&#160;sq&#160;mi)<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The land area of Viking Scotland would have been greater still if place name evidence in Argyll and the south west is taken into account.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although the <i><a href="/wiki/Orkneyinga_Saga" class="mw-redirect" title="Orkneyinga Saga">Orkneyinga Saga</a></i> states that this story is about Raghnall mac Gofraidh it also relates that Ingibj\xc3\xb6rg H\xc3\xa1konard\xc3\xb3ttir, the daughter of Earl <a href="/wiki/Hakon_Paulsson" class="mw-redirect" title="Hakon Paulsson">Hakon Paulsson</a> was his mother. In fact she was the wife of his grandfather King <a href="/wiki/Olaf_I_Godredsson" class="mw-redirect" title="Olaf I Godredsson">Olaf Godredsson</a>. <a href="/wiki/Roger_of_Howden" class="mw-redirect" title="Roger of Howden">Roger of Howden</a>\'s record of these events states that King William actually sought and received the help of Raghnall mac Gofraidh\'s cousin <a href="/wiki/Raghnall_mac_Somhairle" class="mw-redirect" title="Raghnall mac Somhairle">Raghnall mac Somhairle</a>. Williams (2007) suggests that "with regard to Hebridean involvement in Caithness, however, it really does not matter which R\xc3\xb6gnvaldr was involved, so long as he was the grandson of Ingibj\xc3\xb6rg".<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> McDonald (2007) refutes the latter interpretation based on an analysis of Roger of Howden\'s text by <a href="/wiki/A._A._M._Duncan" class="mw-redirect" title="A. A. M. Duncan">A. A. M. Duncan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> </span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-67">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example, Duncan/Donnchad was not a "good old king, but a headstrong young one".<sup id="cite_ref-Mackie43_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mackie43-48">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-158"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-158">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The first recorded bishop was <a href="/wiki/Henry_of_Lund" title="Henry of Lund">Henry of Lund</a> (also known as "the Fat") who was appointed sometime prior to 1035.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> The bishopric appears to have been under the authority of the Archbishops of <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_York" title="Archbishop of York">York</a> and of <a href="/wiki/Archbishopric_of_Bremen" title="Archbishopric of Bremen">Hamburg-Bremen</a> at different times during the early period and from the mid twelfth century to 1472 was subordinate to the <a href="/wiki/Archdiocese_of_Nidaros" class="mw-redirect" title="Archdiocese of Nidaros">Archbishop of Nidaros</a> (today\'s <a href="/wiki/Trondheim" title="Trondheim">Trondheim</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-161"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-161">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The conversion of Scandinavian Scotland and the resultant end to slavery and integration of Viking society into mainstream European culture was a significant event. It took place at an early date, although the popular image of marauding <a href="/wiki/Berserker" title="Berserker">berserkers</a> and of the Norse as "enemies of social progress" remains.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Citations">Citations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Citations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.abdn.ac.uk/history/staff/details.php?id=d.n.dumville">"Professor David Dumville"</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.is/20120801060356/http://www.abdn.ac.uk/history/staff/details.php?id=d.n.dumville">Archived</a> 2012-08-01 at <a href="/wiki/Archive.is" title="Archive.is">Archive.is</a>. id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sellar (2000) p. 193.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-35"><span
